The Universe is Originated and Contingent Due to Change
Dardeer begins the section on divine matters, proving the universe's origin and contingency via change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core kalam argument that the universe is originated and contingent because change is observed in it, and why this implies a Creator.

Core Idea
Change proves contingency, and contingency requires a Creator.

Dardeer begins the section on divine matters by stating a foundational truth: the universe—meaning everything other than God—is originated and contingent. The proof is simple but profound: change occurs in it. This lesson unpacks that argument and its significance in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise, poetic style and the direct proof.
The verse form 'al-'alama' is for rhyme; ignore for meaning.
2
Define key terms: al-‘alam, hadith, muftaqir
Understand that al-‘alam (the universe) includes everything except Allah. Hadith means originated, not eternal. Muftaqir means contingent, depending on a cause.
These terms are foundational for all future arguments in kalam.
3
Analyze the proof: change (taghayyur)
The proof given is simply 'because change occurred in it.' Reflect on why change proves contingency: a being that changes cannot be necessary, as the Necessary Being must be unchanging.
4
Connect to the larger goal
Understand that this argument establishes the need for a Creator. If the universe is contingent, it requires a cause outside itself—that is Allah.
This is the cosmological argument in its simplest form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات
ثم اعلمن بأن هذا العالما * أي ما سوى الله العلي العالما
من غير شك حادث مفتقر * لأنه قام التغير
The first section: Divine Matters. Then know that this universe – i.e., everything besides God, the High, the All-Knowing – is, without doubt, originated and contingent, because change occurred in it.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the section on divine matters (ilahiyyat). The author asserts that the entire universe (al-'alam), defined as everything other than God, is originated (hadith) and contingent (muftaqir) – meaning it depends on a cause for its existence. The proof given is simply 'because change occurred in it' (li-annahu qama al-taghayyur). Change (taghayyur) is a sign of contingency because a being that changes cannot be necessary; it requires a changer. This is a foundational cosmological argument in kalam.

Key Terms
الإلهيات
العالم
حادث
مفتقر
التغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Change does not necessarily imply creation; the universe could be eternal but undergoing change.
Correction: In kalam, change (taghayyur) is a sign of contingency (imkan) because a necessary being cannot change. If something changes, it requires a cause for that change, and ultimately traces back to a Necessary Being who is unchanging. Thus change proves the universe is contingent and therefore originated.

Checkpoint
What is the proof Dardeer gives for the universe being originated?
A) It is composed of parts
B) Change occurs in it
C) It is limited in size
D) It has a beginning in time

Key Takeaway
The universe's change proves it is originated and contingent, pointing to a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your existence is contingent upon God every moment, or do you feel self-sufficient? What would change if you truly believed that any good, any breath, is a gift from the Creator? Commit today to acknowledging your dependence in a specific way—perhaps by saying 'Alhamdulillah' with full presence of heart each time you notice a change in your life.
?
If change proves contingency, and you are part of this changing universe, what does that mean for your own dependence on God? Are you trying to find stability in changing things—wealth, health, relationships—instead of anchoring your heart in the Unchanging One? Take one tangible step today to shift your trust from the created to the Creator.
?
The author says 'know that this universe is originated and contingent.' Do you truly know this? Is that knowledge reflected in your daily actions and reliance on Allah? Set a specific reminder—like a note on your phone or a frequent dhikr—that whenever you witness change, you are reminded of your own contingency and need for Allah.

The World's Temporal Nature and Proof from Change
Sanusi defines the world as created and gives proof from change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational Islamic theological claim that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith) and dependent (muftaqir), with change as the key proof.

Core Idea
Change in the world proves it is not eternal; it must have a beginning.

We begin the first major section of the poem, Ilāhiyyāt (Divine Matters). Before discussing Allah's attributes, the author establishes a crucial premise: the world itself is not eternal or self-sufficient. This passage defines the world, asserts its temporal origination, and presents a simple yet powerful proof: change.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context: start of Ilāhiyyāt
This passage marks the beginning of the first section on divine matters. Recognize the shift from general introduction to specific theological arguments.
2
Grasp the definitions
The world is defined as 'everything other than Allah'. Note the terms ḥādith (temporally originated) and muftaqir (dependent). Understand that ḥudūth means 'existence after non-existence'.
The opposite of ḥudūth is qidam (pre-eternity), which belongs only to Allah.
3
Follow the proof from change
The proof is direct: 'because change has occurred'. Any change indicates contingency; what changes cannot be eternal. Therefore the world is ḥādith and needs a Creator.
Change here is any change, not just substantial change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المنظومة القسم الأول: الإلهيات ثم اعلمن بأن هذا العالما أي ما سوى الله العلي العالما من غير شك حادث مفتقر لأنه قام التغير حدوثه وجوده بعد العدم وضله هو المسمى بالقدم
The text of the poem: The first section: Divine matters (al-ilāhiyyāt). Then know that this world, meaning everything other than God the Most High, the world, is without doubt temporally originated (ḥādith), dependent (muftaqir), because change has occurred. Its origination (ḥudūth) is its existence after non-existence, and its opposite is what is called pre-eternity (al-qidam).
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the first section (ilāhiyyāt) of the poem. It states that the entire world (everything besides God) is temporally originated (ḥādith) and dependent (muftaqir), meaning it needs a creator. The proof given is that change (taghayyur) occurs in the world—change is a sign of temporality. It then defines origination (ḥudūth) as existence after non-existence. The opposite of ḥudūth is pre-eternity (qidam), which is an attribute belonging only to God. The argument is that because change is observed in the world, the world cannot be eternal; it must have a beginning, and thus requires a creator who is eternal.

Key Terms
إلهيات
حادث
مفتقر
التغير
حدوث
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Change only means substantial transformation, like a substance turning into another.
Correction: The passage intends any change at all—even accidental changes like movement, color, or temperature—as evidence of contingency and temporality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what proves the world is temporally originated (ḥādith)?
A) The world's beauty
B) The occurrence of change
C) The world's complexity
D) The existence of prophets

Key Takeaway
The entire universe is temporally originated because change is observed, and it is therefore dependent on an eternal Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since everything around you—your body, your possessions, your relationships—is temporally originated and dependent, what are you clinging to as if it will last forever? How does that affect your relationship with Allah, the only Eternal?
?
Change is the proof that you are not self-sufficient. In what areas of your life do you still resist dependence on Allah, insisting on your own independence despite knowing you are muftaqir?
?
If pre-eternity (qidam) belongs only to Allah, then what have you placed in your heart that you treat as permanent or ultimate—your career, your reputation, your comfort? Will you repent and redirect that devotion to Him alone?

God's Absolute Independence and Refutation of Obligation
Establishes God's independence and refutes obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical Sunni theology affirms God's absolute independence and rejects any notion of obligation upon Him, particularly the Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'doing what is best'.

Core Idea
God is absolutely free—He is not obligated to create, benefit, or punish.

This lesson examines a key passage from the Kharidat al-Bahiyyah that establishes God's complete independence (al-ghinā) and refutes the idea that He must act for the benefit of creation. The poem contrasts the poverty of all creatures with God's self-sufficiency, and then directly condemns those who claim God is obliged to do what is good. Let's explore the reasoning behind this strong censure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the contrast of poverty and wealth
The passage first defines poverty: anything that has something else subsisting in it is in absolute poverty. This establishes that only God is truly independent.
This sets the stage for divine transcendence.
2
Affirm God's absolute freedom in action
The poem then lists actions that are permissible for God: creating, giving offspring, causing misery, bestowing happiness. These are all within His power and not constrained by any external obligation.
This includes both beneficial and harmful acts.
3
Recognize the rejection of obligation
Finally, the passage explicitly censures those who say God must do what is good. This refutes the Mu'tazilite position and affirms that God's actions are not subject to moral obligation.
The term 'discourteous' is strong—it implies a lack of proper adab toward Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكُلُّ مَنْ قامَ بِهِ سِواها * فهو الذي في الفَقْر قد تناهى * والواحِدُ المعبُودُ لا يفتَقِرُ * لغَيرِهِ، جَلَّ الغَنِي الْمُقْتَدِرُ * وجائز في حقه الإيجادُ * والتربُ والإشقاء والإسعادُ * ومَنْ يَقُلْ: فعل الصلاح وجبا * على الإله قد أساء الأدبا
And whoever has something other than Him subsisting in him * it is he who has reached the utmost poverty. * And the One worshipped does not need * anything other than Him, majestic is the Rich, the Powerful. * And permissible in His right is giving existence * and giving offspring, causing misery, and bestowing happiness. * And whoever says: doing what is good is obligatory * upon God has indeed acted discourteously.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage deals with divine independence (al-ghinā) and refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must do what is most beneficial for His creatures (wuǧūb al-ṣalāḥ). It begins by stating that anyone in whom something other than God subsists is in absolute poverty—meaning all creatures depend on God, but God depends on nothing. The One worshipped is free of need for anything else. Then it affirms that it is permissible for God to create, to grant offspring, to cause misery, and to bestow happiness—He is not constrained to benefit only. Finally, it condemns anyone who claims that doing good is obligatory upon God, saying such a person has been discourteous (asā'a al-adab). This is a rejection of the idea that God is obliged to do anything, affirming His absolute sovereignty.

Key Terms
الغني
المقتدر
الإيجاد
التول
الإشقاء
الإسعاد
فعل الصلاح
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to do what is best for His creatures.
Correction: The passage states that God is not obligated to do what is good; He does as He wills, and it is discourteous to impose obligations on Him.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about someone who claims God must do what is good?
A) They are correct.
B) They are being polite.
C) They have acted discourteously.
D) They are showing proper respect.

Key Takeaway
God is absolutely independent and not subject to any obligation; claiming otherwise is a breach of proper respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly believe that God owes you something—health, wealth, or answered prayers—because you have been good? How does this passage strip away that assumption and leave you face to face with His sheer freedom?
?
If God is not obligated to do good for you, then every blessing you have is pure grace. What would change in your daily gratitude if you truly lived this truth?
?
The passage calls it 'discourteous' to impose obligation on God. Where in your heart do you find yourself setting conditions for God—expecting Him to act in a certain way? Will you repent of that discourtesy today?

The Linguistic and Theological Definition of 'Ism' and 'Allāh'
Sanusi defines 'ism' and 'Allah', covering etymology and theological implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and grammatical definition of 'ism' (name), its etymology according to Basran and Kufan grammarians, and the theological meaning of 'Allah' as the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
The word 'ism' points to its named entity, and 'Allah' is the proper name for the Self that must exist.

This lesson unpacks the opening of the Kharida by examining two key terms: 'ism' (name) and 'Allah'. Sanusi first defines 'ism' from linguistic and grammatical perspectives, including its disputed etymology, then explains that in the basmalah it refers to the named entity itself. He then defines 'Allah' with the foundational kalam concept of wājib al-wujūd (the Necessary Existent), setting the stage for arguments about Allah's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the two definitions. Notice the structure: first 'ism', then 'Allah'.
The passage is dense with grammatical terms – focus on the main ideas.
2
Examine the definitions of 'ism'
Identify the linguistic definition (indicates a named thing) and the grammatical definition (indicates a meaning without time). Then compare the two etymologies: Basran (from sumuww, elevation) and Kufan (from sima, mark).
The derivation process is detailed but the key takeaway is that 'ism' is derived from a root meaning 'height' or 'mark'.
3
Understand the theological definition of 'Allah'
Focus on the definition of Allah as wājib al-wujūd (Necessary Existent). Understand the contrast with jā'iz al-wujūd (contingent) and mustahīl al-wujūd (impossible). This is a core concept in kalam.
Note how the definition excludes any possibility of non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الشيخ الدردير والاسمُ لغةً: ما دلَّ على مُسمّى، وعِندَ النُّحاةِ: ما دلَّ على معنى في نفْسِه غَيْرَ مُقْتَرن بزمان وضعًا. وهو مُشْتَقٌ عِندَ البصري مِنَ السُّمو» وهو العلو، لأنه يعلو بهِ مُسَماءِ مِنَ الخَفَاءِ، أَيْ: يظهرُ ، فَأَصْلُه «سِمُوا بِكَسْرِ فَسُكون، فخُفِّفَ بحَذْفِ لامه (۲) أي بحذف الواو التي تقع في الميزان لاما للكلمة.، وعُوِّضَ عنها همزةُ الوَصْل بعد تشكين فائه (۳) أي بعد حذف الواو عوض عنها بهمزة الوصل وسكنت السين - التي تقع في الميزان فاءً للكلمة - فصارت «اسم» بعد أن كانت «سمو».. وعِندَ الكوفي مِنَ «السمة»، وهي العلامة، لأنه علامة على مُسَاه، وأصْلُه وسمٌ، فَخُفِّفَ بحذف فائهِ ثُمَّ عُوِّضَ عنها همزة الوصل (٤) عكس ما في سابقه.. والمراد به هُنا المُسَمّى، أي: مُستَعينًا بمُسَمّى الله، والإضافة للبيان (٥) أي مستعينا بالذات، فتكون الإضافة للبيان، أي: مستعينا باسم هو الله تعالى.. و«الله»: علم على الذات الواجب الوجود (٦) الواجب الوجود أي الثابت الذي لا يقبل الانتفاء أزلا وأبدًا، فمعنى واجب الوجود أنه تعالى لیس جائز الوجود ولا مستحيلا، لأنه لو كان جائز الوجود لقبل الوجود تارة والعدم تارة أخرى، ولو كان مستحيلاً لما قبل الوجود أصلا.، الخالق للعالم.
Introduction of Shaykh al-Dardir. And 'al-ism' (the name) linguistically: what indicates a named thing. According to grammarians: what indicates a meaning in itself without being coupled with time by convention. It is derived according to the Basrans from al-sumuww (elevation) — and it is elevation, because it elevates its named thing from obscurity, i.e., it makes it appear. Its root is 'simw' with a kasra and sukun, then it was lightened by deleting its lam (2) i.e., deleting the waw which is the lam of the word in the meter, and it was compensated by a hamzat al-wasl after fixing its fa (3) i.e., after deleting the waw it was compensated by a hamzat al-wasl and the sin (which is the fa of the word in the meter) was made sakina, so it became 'ism' after it was 'simw'. According to the Kufans, it is derived from al-sima (mark), because it is a mark on its named thing. Its root was 'wasm', then it was lightened by deleting its fa, then compensated by a hamzat al-wasl (4) the opposite of the previous. And what is meant by it here is the named thing (al-musammā), i.e., seeking aid through the named entity of Allah, and the addition (idāfa) is for clarification (5) i.e., seeking aid through the name that is Allah the Exalted. And 'Allah': a proper noun for the self that is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) (6) the necessary of existence is the fixed one that does not admit non-existence eternally and everlastingly. Thus the meaning of 'necessary of existence' is that He is not possible (jā'iz) in existence nor impossible (mustahīl), because if He were possible in existence, He would admit existence sometimes and non-existence other times, and if He were impossible, He would not admit existence at all. — the creator of the world.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage provides linguistic and theological definitions. First, the author cites the lexical meaning of 'ism' (name): anything that indicates a named object. The grammatical definition adds that it indicates a meaning in itself (not requiring a sentence) and is not linked to time by convention. He then gives the etymology according to the two schools: the Basrans derive it from sumuww (height), implying that the name raises the named thing from obscurity; the Kufans derive it from sima (mark), as the name is a distinguishing mark. The root forms and derivations are explained. Then he states that in this context, 'name' refers to the named entity itself (al-musammā), so 'bismillah' means 'with the named entity of Allah'. He then defines 'Allah' as a proper noun for the entity that is wājib al-wujūd (necessary of existence), explaining that 'necessary of existence' means existence that cannot be removed, neither contingent nor impossible. This is a foundational theological concept: Allah is not subject to possibility (jā'iz) or impossibility (mustahīl) because they admit non-existence. Thus the definition moves from linguistic analysis to the core of kalam: proving the Necessary Existent.

Key Terms
الاسم
السمو
السمة
المسمى
الله
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
مستحيل الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'ism' in the basmalah refers to the word 'Allah' as a mere label, distinct from the entity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that in this context, 'ism' means the named entity itself (al-musammā), so 'bismillah' means 'with the entity of Allah'.

Checkpoint
According to the Basrans, the word 'ism' is derived from which root?
A) Sima (mark)
B) Sumuww (elevation)
C) Wasm (brand)
D) Samā' (sky)

Key Takeaway
This lesson shows that 'ism' refers to the named thing, and 'Allah' is the proper name for the Self that necessarily exists, neither contingent nor impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' before an action, do you truly intend to seek aid through the named Being—Allah Himself—or are you just repeating words out of habit? What would change if you pondered the meaning every time?
?
Your own existence is merely possible (jā'iz); you could have not been. Does this realization humble you enough to live each moment in gratitude to the One who necessarily exists and sustains you?
?
If 'Allah' is the proper name of the Self that must exist, and you have been created by Him, then your entire identity is bound to His reality. Are you living as if that is true, or do your daily choices reflect a forgetfulness of this ultimate dependence?

The Meaning of 'Allah' as the Necessary Being
Definition of 'Allah' and explanation of necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'Allah' is a proper noun for the Essence that necessarily exists, and you will grasp the meaning of 'wajib al-wujud' as necessary existence that cannot be non-existent and is neither possible nor impossible.

Core Idea
Allah's existence is necessary, not possible or impossible.

This lesson clarifies the foundational concept of 'wajib al-wujud' (the Necessary Being). The author, Imam Dardir, explains that the name 'Allah' refers to the Essence that necessarily exists, distinguishing it from contingent beings. Understanding this distinction is crucial for all subsequent theological discussions about the nature of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: الله, الواجب الوجود, جائز الوجود, مستحيل.
The footnote in parentheses is part of the explanation.
2
Analyze the definition of 'wajib al-wujud'
Identify the three categories mentioned: wajib (necessary), ja'iz (possible), mustahil (impossible). Understand why the Necessary Being cannot be possible or impossible.
Consider how this distinction applies to all things besides Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد به هُنا المُسَمّى، أي: مُستَعينًا بمُسَمّى الله، والإضافة للبيان. و«الله»: علم على الذات الواجب الوجود، الخالق للعالم. (الواجب الوجود أي الثابت الذي لا يقبل الانتفاء أزلا وأبدًا، فمعنى واجب الوجود أنه تعالى لیس جائز الوجود ولا مستحيلا، لأنه لو كان جائز الوجود لقبل الوجود تارة والعدم تارة أخرى، ولو كان مستحيلاً لما قبل الوجود أصلا.)
And what is meant by 'name' here is the named thing (al-musamma), i.e., seeking help by the named thing which is Allah, and the idafa (genitive construction) is for clarification (al-bayan). And 'Allah' is a proper noun (alam) for the Essence that necessarily exists (al-dhat al-wajib al-wujud), the Creator of the universe. (Al-wajib al-wujud means the established that does not admit non-existence in pre-eternity and post-eternity. The meaning of 'necessarily existing' is that He, exalted, is neither possible (ja'iz al-wujud) nor impossible (mustahil), because if He were possible, He would admit existence at one time and non-existence at another, and if He were impossible, He would not admit existence at all.)
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage clarifies the referent of 'the name' in the basmala: it is the named entity, Allah. The genitive construction (idafa) is for clarification, not possession. Then the author defines 'Allah' as a proper noun (alam) for the Essence that is 'wajib al-wujud' (necessarily existent). The footnote explains this term: it refers to that which is eternally established and cannot ever be non-existent. It is neither possible (ja'iz) nor impossible (mustahil). If it were possible, it would need a cause and could sometimes exist and sometimes not. If it were impossible, it could never exist. So it is necessary, without cause. This is a foundational concept in kalam: distinguishing the Necessary Being from contingent beings.

Key Terms
الله
الذات الواجب الوجود
الواجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'wajib al-wujud' as used in this passage?
A) That which is possible to exist
B) That which is impossible to exist
C) That which necessarily exists, without beginning or end
D) That which is created

Key Takeaway
Allah is the Necessary Being whose existence is inherently eternal and uncaused, distinct from all possible and impossible things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is the Necessary Being,' does your heart tremble at the fact that without His necessary existence, you would be nothing? How often do you let this truth affect your daily choices?
?
If Allah's existence is inherently necessary and uncaused, why do you still turn to created things as if they can give you security? What would it mean to truly rely only on the Necessary Being?
?
Consider one area of your life where you feel self-sufficient. Now repeat: 'Allah is the only Necessary Being, and I am entirely possible.' How does that change your perspective on that area right now?

The Legal Obligation to Know Allah
The author enumerates the legal obligations and defines mukallaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation to know Allah is a legal (shar'i) obligation, not merely rational, and you will know who is considered a mukallaf.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah is a legal duty, not just a rational one, and it applies to every adult sane person who has received the call.

In this passage, the author clarifies a foundational point of Islamic theology: the basis of our duty to know Allah. He affirms that this obligation comes from the Lawgiver, not from reason alone. This sets the Sunni position apart from the Mu'tazila. The lesson also defines who is addressed by this command.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line
Start by reading the poet's line: 'And obligatory by law upon the mukallaf is knowledge of Allah, so know.' This is the core thesis.
The poem uses 'wajib shar'an' to indicate a legal obligation.
2
Understand the legal vs rational debate
The commentary explains that 'wajib shar'an' means obligation derived from the Lawgiver, contrasting with the Mu'tazila who claim it is obligatory by reason. Notice how the author refutes them.
This is a key difference between Sunni and Mu'tazili theology.
3
Define the mukallaf
The text defines mukallaf as the adult, sane person who has received the call of prophecy. Identify each element of this definition.
This definition excludes children, the insane, and those unreached by the message.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: if obligation is legal, there is no duty before revelation. This affects how we understand accountability.
This also distinguishes angels, who are not mukallaf in the same way.

أقسام الحكم العقلي
مُنحت أي أعطيت أي أعطاك الله تعالى لذة أي حلاوة الأفهام بفتح المزة جمع فهم وهو الإدراك أي العلم والمعرفة فإن من أعطي لذة العلوم والمعارف فقد أعطي خيري الدنيا والآخرة.
۱۱ - وواجب شرعًا على المكلّفِ * معرفةُ اللهِ العَلِيِّ ، فاعْرف
وواجب شرعًا أي وجوب شرع فحذف المضاف وأقيم المضاف إليه مقامه (۱) وجوب فانتصب انتصابه فهو منصوب على أنه مفعول مطلق أي وجوبًا مستفادًا من الشرع أي الشارع يعني أنه يجب وجوبًا شرعيًّا خلافًا للمعتزلة القائلين إن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة بالعقل (۲) على المكلف من الثقلين (۳) الإنس والجن. والتكليف إلزام ما فيه كلفة وقيل طلب ما فيه كلفة فلا تكليف بالمندوب والمكروه على الأول الصحيح بخلاف الثاني ولا تكليف بالمباح اتفاقًا. (٤) والمكلف البالغ العاقل الذي بلغته الدعوة. (۱) مقامه بضم الميم لأنه من أقام الرباعي وأما إن كان من مصدر الثلاثي فيقال بفتح الميم يقال قام زيد مقام عمرو. (۲) لا تكليف بالأحكام الشرعية إلا بعد الشرع أي البعثة فلا حكم قبل الشرع لا أصليا ولا فرعيا خلافًا للمعتزلة القائلين بأن معرفته تعالى وجبت بالعقل. (۳) قوله من الثقلين أخرج الملائكة لأن معرفتهم بأحكام الألوهية ضرورية في حقهم فلا يكلفون بها وسمي المكلفون بالثقلين لأنهم ثقلوا بالتكليف. (٤) هذا تعريف للمكلف من الإنس وأما الجن فهم مكلفون من حين الخلقة.
The divisions of rational judgment.
You have been granted, i.e., given, i.e., Allah Almighty has given you the pleasure, i.e., sweetness, of understandings (al-afham) – with fatha on the meem – plural of fahm, which is perception, i.e., knowledge and cognition. For whoever is given the pleasure of sciences and cognitions has indeed been given the good of this world and the Hereafter.
11 – And obligatory by law upon the legally responsible [person] is knowledge of Allah, the Most High, so know.
(And obligatory by law) i.e., an obligation from the Lawgiver. So the adjunct is omitted and the adjunct is made to stand in its place (1) as an obligation, so it is in the accusative as an absolute object, i.e., an obligation derived from the Law, i.e., the Lawgiver, meaning that it is obligatory in a legal sense, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that knowledge of Allah Almighty is obligatory by reason (2). Upon the legally responsible from the two weighty ones (al-thaqalayn) (3): humans and jinn. And legal responsibility (taklif) is: imposing a burden, and it is said: demanding a burden, so there is no legal responsibility for the recommended and the disliked according to the first [definition] which is correct, unlike the second, and there is no legal responsibility for the permissible by consensus. (4) And the legally responsible (mukallaf) is: the adult, sane person who has received the call. (1) 'Muqamuhu' with damma on the meem because it is from the fourth form verb 'aqama', but if it is from the third form verbal noun, it is said with fatha on the meem. It is said: 'Qama Zaydun muqama 'Amrin.' (2) There is no legal responsibility for legal rulings except after the law, i.e., the sending of messengers, so there is no ruling before the law, neither fundamental nor secondary, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that knowledge of Him Almighty is obligatory by reason. (3) His saying 'from the two weighty ones' excludes the angels because their knowledge of the rulings of divinity is necessary in their regard, so they are not charged with them, and the legally responsible are called the two weighty ones because they are burdened with legal responsibility. (4) This is a definition of the mukallaf from among humans; as for the jinn, they are legally responsible from the moment of creation.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage shifts to a new topic: the obligation of knowing Allah. It begins with an invocation: 'You have been granted the pleasure of understandings,' which is an address to the reader or student, encouraging them to seek knowledge. Then the poet's line is cited: 'And obligatory by law upon the legally responsible is knowledge of Allah, the Most High, so know.' The author explains that 'obligatory by law' here means an obligation derived from the Lawgiver, expressed as an absolute object (maf'ul mutlaq). He stresses that this obligation is legal (shar'i), in opposition to the Mu'tazila who hold that knowledge of Allah is obligatory by reason alone. This is a key theological difference: Ahl al-Sunna maintain that before revelation, there is no obligation to know Allah, whereas the Mu'tazila argue it is rationally binding. The author then defines the mukallaf (legally responsible person): the adult, sane individual who has received the call (of prophecy). He notes that the legally responsible are from the two weighty ones (al-thaqalayn): humans and jinn, excluding angels because their knowledge of divine matters is necessary and not acquired through obligation. He then discusses the definition of taklif (legal responsibility): it is imposing a burden, or demanding an action involving burden. Based on the first definition, there is no legal responsibility for recommended (mandub) or disliked (makruh) acts because they do not involve burden in the same way; the second definition would include them. There is consensus that permissible (mubah) acts are not subject to legal responsibility. Footnote (1) clarifies the pronunciation of 'muqamuhu'; footnote (2) reiterates the difference with the Mu'tazila on the role of reason vs. revelation; footnote (3) explains why angels are not included in al-thaqalayn; footnote (4) notes that jinn are considered morally responsible from the moment of their creation.

Key Terms
واجب شرعاً
معرفة الله
مكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing Allah is obligatory by reason alone, independent of revelation.
Correction: The author insists it is a legal obligation, established only through revelation, thus no one is accountable before the message reaches them.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis of the obligation to know Allah?
A) Reason alone
B) Legal command from the Lawgiver
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Natural instinct

Key Takeaway
The duty to know Allah is a legal obligation from revelation, not rational necessity, and it applies to every adult sane person who has received the call.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the duty to know Allah with the same seriousness as the obligation to pray or fast? If not, what does that reveal about your priorities?
?
The Mu'tazila relied on their intellect to determine knowledge of Allah. In what areas of your faith do you subtly rely on your own reasoning over revelation? Will you submit fully to the Lawgiver's command?
?
If you truly believe that knowing Allah is a legal requirement binding on every adult sane person who has received the call, what concrete step will you take today to fulfill that duty?

The Scope of Legal Responsibility and the Two Weighty Ones
The author expounds on taklif and its subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced discussion of taklif including its definition, which acts are excluded, and who the mukallafun are among humans, jinn, and angels.

Core Idea
Legal responsibility is a burden that applies only to humans and jinn, not angels, and only for obligatory and prohibited acts.

This lesson dives deeper into the concept of taklif (legal responsibility). The author clarifies its precise definition, which acts are included, and which beings are subject to it. Special attention is given to the status of angels and jinn, and the refutation of the Mu'tazila on the role of reason is reiterated.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two definitions of taklif
Read the definition: 'taklif is imposing a burden' or 'demanding a burden.' The first definition excludes recommended and disliked acts; the second includes them. The author favors the first.
This affects which acts are considered obligatory.
2
Learn which acts are not subject to taklif
According to the preferred view, there is no legal responsibility for mandub (recommended) and makruh (disliked). Mubah is not subject to obligation by consensus.
Only wajib and haram directly involve taklif in the strict sense.
3
Identify the thaqalayn
The text says the mukallaf is from the two weighty ones: humans and jinn. Understand why angels are excluded because their knowledge is necessary, not acquired through obligation.
The term 'thaqalayn' highlights the burden of accountability.
4
Examine the footnotes
Footnotes clarify: there is no ruling before revelation, angels are not mukallaf, and jinn are responsible from creation. These details reinforce the legal nature of obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are angels not included among the thaqalayn?
A) They are not burdened with legal responsibility.
B) They are not human.
C) Their knowledge is necessary, not acquired.

Legal responsibility is a specific burden that applies only to humans and jinn under defined conditions, not to angels, and only for acts that genuinely involve a burden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have treated a sunnah as if it were an obligation, perhaps judging others for neglecting it. In light of this lesson's distinction between obligatory and recommended acts, how will you recalibrate your own expectations of yourself and others regarding Islamic duties?
?
The passage states there is no ruling before revelation. This means your intellect alone cannot determine what is right or wrong. When you face a moral dilemma, do you instinctively turn to the Quran and Sunnah, or do you rely on your own reasoning? What concrete step will you take to ensure your judgments remain anchored in revelation?
?
Angels are exempt from taklif because their knowledge is necessary, while you must strive through effort and choice. Does this burden feel like a weight or a gift? How will you transform the 'heaviness' of responsibility into a motivation for gratitude and action rather than complaint?

The Divine Obligation to Know God
Dardeer asserts the divine obligation to know God and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation to know Allah is a divine command, not a rational one, and how Dardeer refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God is a divine command, not a rational obligation.

Dardeer begins by stating a foundational principle: knowing Allah is obligatory by divine law. He then clarifies a key disagreement with the Mu'tazila, who assert that this obligation stems from reason alone. Let's see how he establishes the divine origin of this duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage thoroughly
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the grammatical emphasis on 'wajib shar'iyyan'.
2
Understand the grammatical construction
Dardeer explains that 'wajib' is in the accusative as an absolute object, meaning 'an obligation'. This emphasizes that the obligation comes from the divine Lawgiver.
The phrase 'wajib shar'iyyan' is contrasted with 'wajib bi al-'aql'.
3
Identify the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The author explicitly states that his view opposes the Mu'tazila, who hold that knowledge of God is obligatory by reason alone. Understand why this distinction matters in Islamic theology.
This disagreement touches on the source of moral obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وواجب شرعًا على المكلّفِ * معرفةُ اللهِ العَلِيِّ ، فاعْرف - وواجب شرعًا) أي: وُجوبَ شرع، فحُذِفَ المُضافُ وأُقيمَ المُضافُ إليه مُقامَه (۱) وجوب فانتَصَبَ انتصابه، فهو منصوب على أنه مفعولٌ مُطْلَقٌ، أي: وُجوبًا مُستَفادًا من الشَّرع، أي الشارع، يعني أنه يجب وجوبًا شرعيًّا، خِلافًا للمُعْتَزلة القائلين: إِنَّ معرفة الله تعالى واجبة بالعقل (على المُكَلِّفِ مِنَ الثَّقَلَينِ: الإِنسِ والحِنِّ. والتكليفُ: إلزام ما فيه كُلْفَةٌ ، وقيل : طلب ما فيه كُلفةٌ، فلا تكليف بالمندوب والمكروه على الأوَّلِ الصحيح، بخلاف الثاني، ولا تكليف بالمباح اتفاقا. والمُكَلِّفُ: البالغ العاقلُ الذي بلغَتْه الدَّعوة.
And obligated by Divine Law upon the accountable person is knowledge of Allah, the Most High, so know. (And obligated by Divine Law) means: an obligation of Divine Law; the annexed noun was omitted and the annexing noun was put in its place (1) as 'obligation', so it is in the accusative case as an absolute object, meaning an obligation derived from Divine Law, i.e., the Lawgiver, meaning that it is obligatory by a Divine-Law obligation, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that knowledge of Allah the Most High is obligatory by reason. (Upon the accountable person from the two weighty ones: humans and jinn. And taklif (accountability) is: imposing something that involves hardship, and it is also said: demanding something that involves hardship, so there is no taklif for the recommended and the disliked according to the first correct opinion, contrary to the second, and there is no taklif for the permissible by consensus. And the mukallaf (accountable person) is: the mature, sane person whom the call has reached.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation of knowing God and clarifies the nature of taklif. The author begins by quoting the matn (versified text) that states knowing God is wajib shar'iyan (obligated by Divine Law). He then explains the grammatical construction: the word 'wajib' is in the accusative as a masdar maw'iz (absolute object), meaning 'an obligation'. This obligation is from the Shari' (Lawgiver), thus divine in origin. The author immediately contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position that knowledge of God is wajib bi al-'aql (obligatory by reason). Then he specifies that this obligation applies to the mukallaf from the two weighty groups: humans and jinn. Next, he defines taklif: it is imposing something that involves kulfah (hardship or difficulty). He notes a variant definition: demanding something with hardship. Based on the first definition, there is no taklif for mandub (recommended) or makruh (disliked) acts, because they do not involve hardship in the sense of being burdensome? Actually, the first definition excludes mandub and makruh because they are not with hardship, but the second definition might include them. The author states that the first correct view is that there is no taklif for mandub and makruh, but according to the second view there is. There is consensus that there is no taklif for mubah (permissible). Finally, he defines mukallaf: a person who is baligh (mature), 'aqil (sane), and has received the da'wah (call of Islam).

Key Terms
واجب شرعاً
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the obligation to know God is purely a rational duty, independent of divine revelation.
Correction: Dardeer clarifies that the obligation is derived from the Shari'ah (divine law), not from reason, against the Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint
According to Dardeer, the obligation to know Allah is:
A) obligatory by reason alone
B) obligatory by divine command
C) optional
D) necessary by instinct

Key Takeaway
Knowing Allah is a divine command, not a rational obligation, contrary to the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the pursuit of knowing Allah as a personal command from Him, or as an optional intellectual interest? What would change in your daily life if you genuinely believed this knowledge is wajib shar‘iyyan?
?
The Mu'tazila thought reason alone makes knowledge of God obligatory. But the author says it's a command from the Lawgiver. When you seek knowledge of Allah, is your motivation more from a sense of intellectual duty or from submission to His command? What does that reveal about your heart?

The Meaning of Taklif and Who is Accountable
Dardeer defines taklif and specifies the mukallaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taklif (accountability) and the conditions for being a mukallaf (accountable person).

Core Idea
Taklif means imposing hardship, and applies only to mature, sane persons who have received the call.

After establishing the obligation to know God, Dardeer defines who is obligated. He explains the concept of taklif and specifies that it applies to mature, sane humans and jinn who have received the prophetic call. Let's examine the precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the definitions of taklif and the conditions for mukallaf.
Notice the two definitions of taklif and their implications.
2
Analyze the two definitions of taklif
The first definition is 'imposing something with hardship' and the second is 'demanding something with hardship'. Understand how these differ and their rulings on recommended and disliked acts.
The first definition excludes mandub and makruh, while the second includes them.
3
Identify the conditions of the mukallaf
The mukallaf is defined as the mature, sane person who has received the call. Also note that it includes both humans and jinn.
These conditions are standard in Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text

وواجب شرعًا على المكلّفِ * معرفةُ اللهِ العَلِيِّ ، فاعْرف - وواجب شرعًا) أي: وُجوبَ شرع، فحُذِفَ المُضافُ وأُقيمَ المُضافُ إليه مُقامَه (۱) وجوب فانتَصَبَ انتصابه، فهو منصوب على أنه مفعولٌ مُطْلَقٌ، أي: وُجوبًا مُستَفادًا من الشَّرع، أي الشارع، يعني أنه يجب وجوبًا شرعيًّا، خِلافًا للمُعْتَزلة القائلين: إِنَّ معرفة الله تعالى واجبة بالعقل (على المُكَلِّفِ مِنَ الثَّقَلَينِ: الإِنسِ والحِنِّ. والتكليفُ: إلزام ما فيه كُلْفَةٌ ، وقيل : طلب ما فيه كُلفةٌ، فلا تكليف بالمندوب والمكروه على الأوَّلِ الصحيح، بخلاف الثاني، ولا تكليف بالمباح اتفاقا. والمُكَلِّفُ: البالغ العاقلُ الذي بلغَتْه الدَّعوة.
And obligated by Divine Law upon the accountable person is knowledge of Allah, the Most High, so know. (And obligated by Divine Law) means: an obligation of Divine Law; the annexed noun was omitted and the annexing noun was put in its place (1) as 'obligation', so it is in the accusative case as an absolute object, meaning an obligation derived from Divine Law, i.e., the Lawgiver, meaning that it is obligatory by a Divine-Law obligation, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that knowledge of Allah the Most High is obligatory by reason. (Upon the accountable person from the two weighty ones: humans and jinn. And taklif (accountability) is: imposing something that involves hardship, and it is also said: demanding something that involves hardship, so there is no taklif for the recommended and the disliked according to the first correct opinion, contrary to the second, and there is no taklif for the permissible by consensus. And the mukallaf (accountable person) is: the mature, sane person whom the call has reached.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation of knowing God and clarifies the nature of taklif. The author begins by quoting the matn (versified text) that states knowing God is wajib shar'iyan (obligated by Divine Law). He then explains the grammatical construction: the word 'wajib' is in the accusative as a masdar maw'iz (absolute object), meaning 'an obligation'. This obligation is from the Shari' (Lawgiver), thus divine in origin. The author immediately contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position that knowledge of God is wajib bi al-'aql (obligatory by reason). Then he specifies that this obligation applies to the mukallaf from the two weighty groups: humans and jinn. Next, he defines taklif: it is imposing something that involves kulfah (hardship or difficulty). He notes a variant definition: demanding something with hardship. Based on the first definition, there is no taklif for mandub (recommended) or makruh (disliked) acts, because they do not involve hardship in the sense of being burdensome? Actually, the first definition excludes mandub and makruh because they are not with hardship, but the second definition might include them. The author states that the first correct view is that there is no taklif for mandub and makruh, but according to the second view there is. There is consensus that there is no taklif for mubah (permissible). Finally, he defines mukallaf: a person who is baligh (mature), 'aqil (sane), and has received the da'wah (call of Islam).

Key Terms

المكلف
المندوب
المكروه
المباح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all religious duties (like recommended acts) are equally obligatory and involve taklif.
Correction: According to the correct definition, taklif only applies to actions that inherently involve hardship; recommended acts are not included.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the mukallaf is defined as:
A) any human being
B) a mature, sane person who has received the call
C) only prophets
D) all Muslims

Key Takeaway
Taklif applies only to actions with hardship, and a mukallaf must be mature, sane, and reached by the call.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that accountability applies only to those who are mature, sane, and have received the call. Are you living as if you truly believe that you are a mukallaf? In what areas of your life do you act as if you are not accountable to God?
?
The lesson states that taklif involves hardship. When you face difficulty in fulfilling your religious duties, do you embrace it as the nature of accountability, or do you resent God for it? What does your response reveal about your understanding of taklif?
?
The call has reached you personally. Have you responded to it with the seriousness it demands, or do you treat it as just another piece of information? What concrete step will you take today to live as a true mukallaf?

Knowledge of God as the First Obligation
Dardeer states knowledge of God is the first obligation and reveals its source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of God is the primary duty and that its obligation comes from revelation, not reason alone.

Core Idea
The first duty is to know God, and that duty is revealed, not self-evident.

Dardeer's opening lines settle two foundational questions: what is the very first thing a Muslim must do, and where does that command come from? This passage answers both, pointing to knowledge of God as the basis for all other obligations and grounding that duty in divine revelation rather than human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to understand its plain meaning.
Notice the concise style of the author.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two assertions: one about what the first obligation is, and another about the source of that obligation. Find each one.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazilite view
The passage explicitly rejects the Mu'tazilite position. Reflect on why this distinction matters for Islamic theology.
The Mu'tazila held that reason alone makes knowledge of God obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المعرفة هي أول واجب على المكلف، إذ جميع الواجبات متوقفة عليها. وقوله (فاعرف) أي: اعرف أنها واجبة بالشرع لا بالعقل، خلافًا للمعتزلة.
And know that knowledge (al-maʻrifah) is the first obligation upon the morally responsible person (al-mukallaf), since all obligations depend upon it. And His statement 'So know' (fa-iʻrif) means: know that it is obligatory by the Sacred Law (al-sharʻ), not by reason (al-ʻaql), contrary to the Muʻtazila.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage makes two key points. First, it states that knowledge (ma'rifah) of God is the first and primary duty of every mukallaf (legally responsible person). The reason given is that all other obligations depend upon it—without knowing God, one cannot fulfil other duties. Second, it clarifies the basis for this obligation. The verse 'So know' (fa-iʻrif) indicates that the obligation is derived from the divine command, i.e., from revelation (shar'), not from reason alone. This is a direct refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that the obligation to know God is rationally binding even without revelation. Thus the Ash'ari stance is that the obligation is legal (shar'ī) and not purely rational.

Key Terms
المعرفة
أول واجب
المكلف
الشرع
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the duty to know God is self-evident by reason alone.
Correction: The author emphasizes that it is obligatory by the Sacred Law, not by reason alone, contrary to the Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the obligation to know God is established by:
A) Pure reason
B) The Sacred Law
C) Both reason and revelation equally
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God is the first obligation, and its binding force comes from revelation, not reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The obligation comes from revelation, not your own reasoning. Does your heart submit to what Allah has commanded, or do you rely on your own logic to determine what is required of you?
?
You have just learned that all other obligations depend on knowing God. Reflect on your daily acts of worship: do you perform them with a consciousness of who you are worshipping, or have they become mere routines? What will you do to renew that knowledge?

Proof of the World's Origination and Dependence
Imam Dardeer presents the syllogism proving the world is created and needs a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam contingency argument based on the world's changeability, and how it establishes the world's dependence on a Creator.

Core Idea
Change in the world proves it is created, and everything created needs a Creator.

Imam Dardeer begins by stating the core premise of the kalam cosmological argument: the world is undeniably originated (hadith) and dependent (muftaqir) on a Creator. He then breaks down the terms and reconstructs the implicit syllogism. The proof for origination lies in the observable fact of change. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the poetic line and the commentary that follows.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand key terms
Identify the definitions of shakk (doubt), hadith (originated), and muftaqir (dependent). Why does the author expand the meaning of 'doubt'?
The expansion is to preempt objections from philosophers.
3
Reconstruct the syllogism
Find the minor premise (world is originated), the major premise (every originated thing depends on an originator), and the conclusion (world depends on a Creator). Notice the major premise is omitted in the poem but stated in the commentary.
4
Examine the proof via change
Understand why the occurrence of change (taghayyur) in the world proves its origination. Then note the definitions of substance and accident to see how change is universal.
Change implies temporality because eternal things cannot change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مِنْ غَير شَكٍّ حادثٌ مُفْتَقِرُ * لأنهُ قامَ بِهِ التغير - (شَكٍّ) مُتعلّق بقوله: (حادتٌ) أي: مَوجودٌ بعْدَ عدَم، وهو خبَرُ أَنَّ) أي: أَنَّ حُدوثه غَيرُ مشكوك فيه لَنْ تَأَمَّلَ، أَو أَنَّ الْمُرَادَ: أَنه يُحِبُّ له الحدوتُ كَمَا يُحِبُّ مُحْدِثِه القِدَمُ، فلا يَردُ أَنَّ حُدوثه لا يقولُ به الفلسَفيُّ. وحقيقةُ الشكِّ: التردُّدُ في الطرفين على السواء، ومُراده به هُنا مُطلَقُ الترَدُّدِ الشامل للظن، وهو الطرف الراجح، والوهم، وهو المرجوح. (مُفْتَقِر) إلى مُوجِدٍ يوجِدُه مِنَ العَدَم، وهو خبر ثان لازمٌ للأَوَّلِ، إذ الحادثُ لا يكون إلا مُفتقرًا ابتداءً ودَوامًا، وفي الحقيقة هو يُشير إلى نتيجة القياس الذي صرَّحَ بصغراه وطوى كُبْراه، ونظمه هكذا: العالم حادث، وكُلّ حادث فهو مُفتقِرُ مُحدث، ينتج : العالم مفتقر إلى مُحدث. أما دليلُ كَونِ العالم حادِثًا فـ (لأنه قام به) أي: العالم، يعني باعتبار بعضه، وهو الأعراضُ (التغيرُ) مِنْ عَدَم إلى وُجودِ، وَمِنْ وُجودِ إلى عدم، وذلك: (۱) الجوهر في تعريف المتكلمين هو الحادث المتحيز بالذات والعرض هو الموجود القائم بالمتحيز [نشر الطوالع] والأعراض بعضها يدرك بحاسة البصر كالبياض والسواد، وبعضها يدرك بالحواس الأخرى مثل السمع واللمس والذوق، وبعضها يدرك بالعقل كالقدرة في العبد، فعلم من هذا أن الأعراض ليست خاصة بما هو مشاهد.
Without doubt, it is temporally originated and dependent, because change occurred in it. (Doubt) is attached to his saying: (originated) i.e., existing after non-existence, and it is the predicate of 'that' i.e., its origination is not doubtful if you reflect, or the meaning is that it deserves origination just as its originator deserves eternity, so it is not objected that the philosopher does not affirm its origination. The reality of doubt is hesitation between two sides equally, but his intention here is absolute hesitation including conjecture, which is the predominant side, and illusion, which is the weaker side. (Dependent) upon a bringer into existence who brings it from non-existence, and it is a second predicate necessary for the first, for the temporally originated cannot but be dependent initially and perpetually, and in fact it points to the conclusion of the syllogism whose minor premise he stated and whose major premise he omitted, and its formulation is: the world is temporally originated, and every temporally originated thing is dependent upon an originator, resulting: the world is dependent upon an originator. As for the proof that the world is temporally originated, it is because change occurred in it, i.e., the world, meaning by considering some of it, namely the accidents, (change) from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. And that: (1) the substance (jawhar) according to the theologians' definition is the temporally originated which is self-occupying space, and the accident (ʿaraḍ) is the existent subsisting in what occupies space [from Nashr al-Ṭawāliʿ]. Some accidents are perceived by the sense of sight like whiteness and blackness, some by other senses like hearing, touch, and taste, and some are perceived by the intellect like power in the servant. So it is known from this that accidents are not confined to what is observable.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage provides the core kalam argument for the existence of God: the contingency argument based on origination (ḥudūth). The author begins with the poetic line that the world is 'without doubt temporally originated and dependent' because change occurs within it. He then explains 'doubt' (shakk) technically as equal hesitation, but here it means any hesitation including conjecture (ẓann) and illusion (wahm). 'Originated' (ḥādith) means existing after non-existence. The word 'dependent' (muftaqir) indicates the world's need for a creator who brings it from non-existence, a need that is both initial and perpetual. The author reconstructs the implicit syllogism: the world is originated; every originated thing depends on an originator; therefore the world depends on an originator. The proof for the world's origination is that it undergoes change (taghayyur) — accidents appear and disappear. Change implies temporality because that which changes cannot be eternal. The definitions of substance (jawhar) and accident (ʿaraḍ) are given: substance is a temporally originated entity that occupies space by itself; accident is an existent that subsists in a substance. Accidents are perceptible by senses (e.g., colors by sight, sounds by hearing) or by intellect (e.g., human power). The argument concludes that accidents are not limited to observable phenomena, reinforcing the universality of the proof.

Key Terms
حادث
مفتقر
شك
القياس
جوهر
عرض
تغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The kalam argument assumes atheism or begins from a neutral starting point.
Correction: The argument builds on a premise the opponent already accepts: the world is changeable. It does not assume atheism; it uses common observation to deduce the need for a creator.

Checkpoint
How does the author prove that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith)?
A) By asserting it without evidence
B) By observing that it undergoes change (taghayyur)
C) By quoting scripture
D) By consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The world's changeability proves it is temporally originated, and every originated thing requires a creator, hence the world depends on Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience change every day—your mood, your health, your circumstances. If change proves you are not self-existent, then your very dependence on Allah is not just a doctrine but a reality every moment. In what area of your life are you trying to be self-sufficient right now? What would it look like to consciously depend on Allah in that area starting today?
?
The text says the world depends on an originator both initially and perpetually. Your existence is sustained by Allah at every instant. Does your prayer reflect that constant need? Or do you pray only when you feel desperate? This week, try to pause before each action and silently acknowledge your need for Allah to make it possible.
?
If the proof from change is sound, then every change you witness—from the sunrise to your own heartbeat—points directly to the Creator. How often do you let these signs lead you to gratitude and humility? Commit today to noticing one change around you and turning that observation into a moment of thankfulness to Allah.

The Proof of Change in Accidents
Dardeer proves accidents are originated through observable and inferential change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how change in accidents—whether directly observed or logically inferred—proves that accidents are originated and not eternal.

Core Idea
Change proves origination: what can change cannot be eternal.

Imam Dardeer begins the proof of the world's origination by focusing on accidents—the changing qualities of things. He shows that change is either directly observed (like motion after rest) or logically inferred from the uniformity of bodies. The possibility of non-existence rules out eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm. Notice the structured argument starting with 'أما دليلُ كون العالم حادثا'.
2
Identify the two forms of evidence
The passage presents two ways change is known: by observation (المشاهدة) and by evidence (الدليل). Identify examples for each.
Observation examples: motion after rest, light after darkness. Evidence: bodies that appear permanently still or moving could possibly be otherwise.
3
Understand the reasoning from possibility to non-eternity
The argument moves from 'it is possible for change to occur' to 'if change is possible, then non-existence is possible' to 'if non-existence is possible, then eternity is impossible'. Follow this logical chain.
The key phrase: 'وإذا جازَ عدَمُها استحالَ قِدَمُها'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما دليلُ كَونِ العالم حادِثًا فـ (لأنه قام به) أي: العالم، يعني باعتبار بعضه، وهو الأعراضُ (التغيرُ) مِنْ عَدَم إلى وُجودِ، وَمِنْ وُجودِ إلى عدم، وذلك: اما بالمُشاهَدَةِ: كالحَرَكَةِ بعدَ السُّكون، والصّوءِ بعدَ الظَّلْمَةِ، والسَّوادِ بعد البياض، والحرارة بعد البرودة، إلى غير ذلك، والعكس. وإما بالدليل: وذلك لأنَّ ما شُوهِدَ سُكونُه مَثَلًا على الدوام كالجبال، أو حركته على الدوام كالكَواكِبِ جازَ أَنْ يَثبُتَ لَه العَكْسُ، إذ لا فَرْقَ بَينَ جِرْمٍ وَجِرْمٍ. وإذا جازَ عدَمُها استحالَ قِدَمُها ؛ لأنَّ ما ثبَتَ عَدَمُه استحالَ قِدَمُه، فتكون حادثةً، فحينئذ جميع الأعراض حادثة، ويلزَمُ مِنْ حُدوثِها حُدوثُ جميع الأجرام والجواهر؛ لعَدَم انفكاكها عنِ الأعراض الحادثة، وكُلُّ ما لا يَنْفَثُ عَنِ الحادثِ فهو حادث. لمْ يَكُنْ فظهَرَ أَنَّ جميعَ العالَم مِنْ أعراضه وأجْرامِه وجواهره حادث، أي: موجودٌ بعدَ أَنْ لمْ يَكُنْ (۱) نتيجة ما سبق أن الأعراض قائمة بالجواهر لا تنفك عنها، فما من عرض إلا وهو قائم بجوهره، وأن الجواهر ملازمة لتلك الأعراض الحادثة - ودليل حدوثها تغيرها - فدل ذلك على أن الجواهر حادثة كالأعراض، إذ ما قامت به الحوادث ولازمها فهو حادث، والحادث ما سبقه العدم.
As for the proof that the world is originated, it is (because change occurred in it), i.e., the world, meaning considering some of it, namely the accidents: change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. This is either by observation: like motion after rest, light after darkness, blackness after whiteness, heat after cold, etc., and the reverse. Or by evidence: because that whose rest is observed perpetually, like mountains, or whose motion is observed perpetually, like celestial bodies, it is possible for the opposite to occur, since there is no difference between one body and another. And if their non-existence is possible, then their eternity is impossible; because that whose non-existence is established, its eternity is impossible, so they are originated. Then all accidents are originated, and from their origination follows the origination of all bodies and substances, because they are inseparable from originated accidents, and whatever is not free from originated things is itself originated. So it became clear that the entire world—its accidents, bodies, and substances—is originated, i.e., existent after it was not. (1) The result of the foregoing: accidents subsist in substances and are inseparable from them; there is no accident except that it subsists in its substance, and substances are inseparable from those originated accidents—and the proof of their origination is their change—so that indicates that substances are originated like accidents, for whatever is characterized by originated things and is inseparable from them is originated, and the originated is that which is preceded by non-existence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof of the world's origination (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) based on the observed change in accidents. The author begins by stating that the proof is that change occurs in the world, specifically in accidents. Change can be directly observed (e.g., motion after rest, light after darkness) or inferred by evidence: even if some bodies like mountains appear permanently still, it is possible for them to move because there is no inherent difference between bodies. Since it is possible for their accidents to cease, they cannot be eternal; thus all accidents are originated. Then, because substances (jawāhir) are never free from accidents, and accidents are originated, the substances must also be originated. The reasoning is that whatever is inseparable from something originated is itself originated. The footnote (1) reiterates this conclusion: substances are inseparable from originated accidents, so they too are originated. The overall conclusion is that the entire world—accidents, bodies, and substances—is originated, meaning it came into existence after non-existence.

Key Terms
تغير
مشاهدة
دليل
عرض
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that only observable changes count as evidence for origination, and that things appearing permanent (like mountains) could be eternal.
Correction: The passage includes logical inference: even if something appears permanent, it is possible for it to change because all bodies are uniform. This possibility of change suffices to prove origination.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of change known by observation?
A) The possibility of mountains moving
B) Motion after rest
C) The uniformity of bodies
D) The impossibility of eternity

Key Takeaway
Change in accidents, whether observed or inferred, proves that accidents are not eternal but originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that everything that can change cannot be eternal. Your own body changes daily—age, health, moods. If all these accidents are transient, what are you truly building your life upon? Are you anchoring your hope in what will fade?
?
Think of something you consider 'permanent' in your life—a relationship, a possession, a status. The proof argues that even seemingly constant things (like mountains) are rationally possible to change. How would your priorities shift if you truly believed that nothing in this world is immune to decay?
?
The proof uses rational inference to affirm Allah's power over all change. When you notice a small change in your life—a disappointment, a loss, a shift—do you see it as a sign that only Allah is eternal? Or do you still cling to the illusion that some things are beyond change?

From Accidents to Substances: The World's Origination
Dardeer infers the origination of substances from their inseparability from originated accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the origination of accidents necessarily implies the origination of all substances and the entire world.

Core Idea
Whatever is inseparable from something originated is itself originated.

Having established that all accidents are originated, Imam Dardeer now extends the proof to substances (jawāhir). He argues that because substances are never free from accidents, and accidents are originated, substances must also be originated. This completes the proof that the entire world—accidents, bodies, and substances—is originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic
Read the passage from 'فتكون حادثةً' onwards. Notice how it connects the previous conclusion to the next step.
The passage uses 'ف' to show consequence.
2
Identify the key premise
The key premise is: substances (jawāhir) are inseparable from accidents. Find where this is stated.
Look for 'لأنها لا تنفك عن الأعراض' or similar.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: accidents are originated → substances are inseparable from accidents → therefore substances are originated → the whole world is originated.
The phrase 'وكل ما لا ينفك عن الحادث فهو حادث' summarizes the principle.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the entire world—its accidents, bodies, and substances—is originated, meaning it exists after non-existence.
Compare with the initial claim that the world is originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما دليلُ كَونِ العالم حادِثًا فـ (لأنه قام به) أي: العالم، يعني باعتبار بعضه، وهو الأعراضُ (التغيرُ) مِنْ عَدَم إلى وُجودِ، وَمِنْ وُجودِ إلى عدم، وذلك: اما بالمُشاهَدَةِ: كالحَرَكَةِ بعدَ السُّكون، والصّوءِ بعدَ الظَّلْمَةِ، والسَّوادِ بعد البياض، والحرارة بعد البرودة، إلى غير ذلك، والعكس. وإما بالدليل: وذلك لأنَّ ما شُوهِدَ سُكونُه مَثَلًا على الدوام كالجبال، أو حركته على الدوام كالكَواكِبِ جازَ أَنْ يَثبُتَ لَه العَكْسُ، إذ لا فَرْقَ بَينَ جِرْمٍ وَجِرْمٍ. وإذا جازَ عدَمُها استحالَ قِدَمُها ؛ لأنَّ ما ثبَتَ عَدَمُه استحالَ قِدَمُه، فتكون حادثةً، فحينئذ جميع الأعراض حادثة، ويلزَمُ مِنْ حُدوثِها حُدوثُ جميع الأجرام والجواهر؛ لعَدَم انفكاكها عنِ الأعراض الحادثة، وكُلُّ ما لا يَنْفَثُ عَنِ الحادثِ فهو حادث. لمْ يَكُنْ فظهَرَ أَنَّ جميعَ العالَم مِنْ أعراضه وأجْرامِه وجواهره حادث، أي: موجودٌ بعدَ أَنْ لمْ يَكُنْ (۱) نتيجة ما سبق أن الأعراض قائمة بالجواهر لا تنفك عنها، فما من عرض إلا وهو قائم بجوهره، وأن الجواهر ملازمة لتلك الأعراض الحادثة - ودليل حدوثها تغيرها - فدل ذلك على أن الجواهر حادثة كالأعراض، إذ ما قامت به الحوادث ولازمها فهو حادث، والحادث ما سبقه العدم.
As for the proof that the world is originated, it is (because change occurred in it), i.e., the world, meaning considering some of it, namely the accidents: change from non-existence to existence and from existence to non-existence. This is either by observation: like motion after rest, light after darkness, blackness after whiteness, heat after cold, etc., and the reverse. Or by evidence: because that whose rest is observed perpetually, like mountains, or whose motion is observed perpetually, like celestial bodies, it is possible for the opposite to occur, since there is no difference between one body and another. And if their non-existence is possible, then their eternity is impossible; because that whose non-existence is established, its eternity is impossible, so they are originated. Then all accidents are originated, and from their origination follows the origination of all bodies and substances, because they are inseparable from originated accidents, and whatever is not free from originated things is itself originated. So it became clear that the entire world—its accidents, bodies, and substances—is originated, i.e., existent after it was not. (1) The result of the foregoing: accidents subsist in substances and are inseparable from them; there is no accident except that it subsists in its substance, and substances are inseparable from those originated accidents—and the proof of their origination is their change—so that indicates that substances are originated like accidents, for whatever is characterized by originated things and is inseparable from them is originated, and the originated is that which is preceded by non-existence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof of the world's origination (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) based on the observed change in accidents. The author begins by stating that the proof is that change occurs in the world, specifically in accidents. Change can be directly observed (e.g., motion after rest, light after darkness) or inferred by evidence: even if some bodies like mountains appear permanently still, it is possible for them to move because there is no inherent difference between bodies. Since it is possible for their accidents to cease, they cannot be eternal; thus all accidents are originated. Then, because substances (jawāhir) are never free from accidents, and accidents are originated, the substances must also be originated. The reasoning is that whatever is inseparable from something originated is itself originated. The footnote (1) reiterates this conclusion: substances are inseparable from originated accidents, so they too are originated. The overall conclusion is that the entire world—accidents, bodies, and substances—is originated, meaning it came into existence after non-existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that only accidents are originated, while substances (like the atoms) are eternal.
Correction: The passage explicitly argues that because substances are never free from originated accidents, they too are originated. The principle 'whatever is inseparable from originated things is originated' applies directly.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason that substances are proved to be originated?
A) They are observed to change
B) They are inseparable from originated accidents
C) They are eternal by nature
D) They are made of atoms

Key Takeaway
Substances are originated because they are never free from originated accidents, completing the proof that the entire world is originated.

?
The proof shows that nothing in the universe is self-sufficient. In what area of your life do you need to acknowledge your absolute dependence on the Creator today, and what concrete step can you take to express that dependence?
